may within the bound of californium mountain be ever in sight charming and glorifying every landscape yet so simple and massive be the topography of the state in general view that the main central portion display only one valley and two chain of mountain which seem almost perfectly regular in trend and height : the coast range on the west side the sierra nevada on the east these two range come together in curve on the north and south inclose a magnificent basin with a level floor more than 400 mile long and from 35 to 60 mile wide this be the grand central valley of californium the water of which have only one outlet to the sea through the golden gate but with this general simplicity of feature there be great complexity of hidden detail the coast range rise as a grand green barrier against the ocean from 2000 to 8000 foot high be compose of innumerable forest-crowned spur ridge and roll hill-wave which inclose a multitude of smaller valley some look out through long forest-lined vista to the sea other with but few tree to the central valley while a thousand other yet smaller be embosome and conceal in mild round-browed hill each with its own climate soil and production make you way through the maze of the coast range to the summit of any of the inner peak or pass opposite san francisco in the clear springtime the grandest and most tell of all californium landscape be outspread before you at you foot lie the great central valley glowing golden in the sunshine extend north and south farther than the eye can reach one smooth flowery lake-like bed of fertile soil along its eastern margin rise the mighty sierra mile in height repose like a smooth cumulous cloud in the sunny sky and so gloriously color and so luminous it seem to be not clothe with light but wholly compose of it like the wall of some celestial city along the top and extend a good way down you see a pale pearl-gray belt of snow and below it a belt of blue and dark purple mark the extension of the forest and along the base of the range a broad belt of rose-purple and yellow where lie the minor 's gold-field and the foot-hill garden all these color belt blend smoothly make a wall of light ineffably fine and as beautiful as a rainbow yet firm as adamant when i first enjoy this superb view one glowing April day from the summit of the pacheco pass the central valley but little trampled or plow as yet be one furred rich sheet of golden composita and the luminous wall of the mountain shine in all its glory then it seem to I the sierra should be call not the nevada or snowy range but the range of light and after ten year spend in the heart of it rejoice and wonder bathing in its glorious flood of light see the sunburst of morning among the icy peak the noonday radiance on the tree and rock and snow the flush of the alpenglow and a thousand dash waterfall with they marvelous abundance of irised spray it still seem to I above all other the range of light the most divinely beautiful of all the mountain-chain i have ever see the sierra be about 500 mile long 70 mile wide and from 7000 to nearly 15000 foot high in general view no mark of man be visible on it nor anything to suggest the richness of the life it cherish or the depth and grandeur of its sculpture none of its magnificent forest-crowned ridge rise much above the general level to publish its wealth no great valley or lake be see or river or group of well-marked feature of any kind stand out in distinct picture even the summit-peak so clear and high in the sky seem comparatively smooth and featureless nevertheless glacier be still at work in the shadow of the peak and thousand of lake and meadow shine and bloom beneath they and the whole range be furrow with cañons to a depth of from 2000 to 5000 foot in which once flow majestic glacier and in which now flow and sing a band of beautiful river though of such stupendous depth these famous cañons be not raw gloomy jagged-walled gorge savage and inaccessible with rough passage here and there they still make delightful pathway for the mountaineer conduct from the fertile lowland to the highest icy fountain as a kind of mountain street full of charming life and light grade and sculpture by the ancient glacier and present throughout all they course a rich variety of novel and attractive scenery the most attractive that have yet be discover in the mountain-range of the world in many place especially in the middle region of the western flank of the range the main cañons widen into spacious valley or park diversify like artificial landscape-garden with charming grove and meadow and thicket of bloom bush while the lofty retire wall infinitely vary in form and sculpture be fringe with fern flowering-plant of many species oak and evergreen which find anchorage on a thousand narrow step and bench while the whole be enliven and make glorious with rejoice stream that come dancing and foam over the sunny brow of the cliff to join the shine river that flow in tranquil beauty down the middle of each one of they the wall of these park valley of the yosemite kind be make up of rock mountain in size partly separate from each other by narrow gorge and side-cañons and they be so sheer in front and so compactly build together on a level floor that comprehensively see the park they inclose look like immense hall or temple light from above every rock seem to glow with life some lean back in majestic repose other absolutely sheer or nearly so for thousand of foot advance they brow in thoughtful attitude beyond they companion give welcome to storm and calm alike seemingly conscious yet heedless of everything go on about they awful in stern majesty type of permanence yet associate with beauty of the frailest and most fleeting form they foot set in pine-grove and gay emerald meadow they brow in the sky bath in light bath in flood of singing water while snow-clouds avalanche and the wind shine and surge and wreathe first snow that whiten the sierra usually fall about the end of October or early in November to a depth of a few inch after month of the most charming indian summer weather imaginable but in a few day this light cover mostly melt from the slope expose to the sun and cause but little apprehension on the part of mountaineer who may be linger among the high peak at this time the first general winter storm that yield snow that be to form a lasting portion of the season 's supply seldom break on the mountain before the end of November then warn by the sky caution mountaineer together with the wild sheep deer and most of the bird and bear make haste to the lowland or foot-hill and burrow marmot mountain beaver wood-rat and such people go into winter quarter some of they not again to see the light of day until the general awakening and resurrection of the spring in June or July the first heavy fall be usually from about two to four foot in depth then with interval of splendid sunshine storm succeed storm heaping snow on snow until thirty to fifty foot have fall but on account of its settling and compact and the almost constant waste from melting and evaporation the average depth actually find at any time seldom exceed ten foot in the forest region or fifteen foot along the slope of the summit peak even during the coldest weather evaporation never wholly cease and the sunshine that abound between the storm be sufficiently powerful to melt the surface more or less through all the winter month waste from melting also go on to some extent on the bottom from heat store up in the rock and give off slowly to the snow in contact with they as be show by the rising of the stream on all the higher region after the first snowfall and they steady sustained flow all winter the greater portion of the snow deposit around the lofty summit of the range fall in small crisp flake and broken crystal or when accompany by strong wind and low temperature the crystal instead of be lock together in they fall to form tufted flake be beat and break into meal and fine dust but down in the forest region the greater portion come gently to the ground light and feathery some of the flake in mild weather be nearly a inch in diameter and it be evenly distribute and keep from drift to any great extent by the shelter afford by the large tree every tree during the progress of gentle storm be load with fairy bloom at the coldest and darkest time of year bend the branch and hush every singing needle but as soon as the storm be over and the sun shine the snow at once begin to shift and settle and fall from the branch in miniature avalanche and the white forest soon become green again the snow on the ground also settle and thaw every bright day and freeze at night until it become coarsely granulated and lose every trace of its ray crystalline structure and then a man may walk firmly over its frozen surface as if on ice the forest region up to a elevation of 7000 foot be usually in great part free from snow in June but at this time the higher region be still heavy-laden and be not touch by spring weather to any considerable extent before the middle or end of July one of the most striking effect of the snow on the mountain be the burial of the river and small lake as the snow fa 's in the river a moment white then lose forever sing burn in illustrate the fleeting character of human pleasure the first snowflake that fall into the sierra river vanish thus suddenly but in great storm when the temperature be low the abundance of the snow at length chill the water nearly to the freezing-point and then of course it cease to melt and consume the snow so suddenly the fall flake and crystal form cloud-like mass of blue sludge which be sweep forward with the current and carry down to warmer climate many mile distant while some be lodge against log and rock and project point of the bank and last for day pile high above the level of the water and show white again instead of be at once lose forever while the river themselves be at length lose for month during the snowy period the snow be first build out from the bank in bossy over-curling drift compact and cement until the stream be span they then flow in the dark beneath a continuous cover across the snowy zone which be about thirty mile wide all the sierra river and they tributary in these high region be thus lose every winter as if another glacial period have come on not a drop of run water be to be see except at a few point where large fall occur though the rush and rumble of the heavier current may still be hear toward spring when the weather be warm during the day and frosty at night repeat thawing and freezing and new layer of snow render the bridging-mass dense and firm so that one may safely walk across the stream or even lead a horse across they without danger of fall through in June the thinnest part of the winter ceiling and those most expose to sunshine begin to give way form dark rugged-edged pit-like sink at the bottom of which the rush water may be see at the end of June only here and there may the mountaineer find a secure snow-bridge the most lasting of the winter and glacier-sculptured rock when a mountain lake be bear -- when like a young eye it first open to the light -- it be a irregular expressionless crescent inclose in bank of rock and ice -- bare glaciated rock on the lower side the rugged snout of a glacier on the upper in this condition it remain for many a year until at length toward the end of some auspicious cluster of season the glacier recede beyond the upper margin of the basin leave it open from shore to shore for the first time thousand of year after its conception beneath the glacier that excavate its basin the landscape cold and bare be reflect in its pure depths the wind ruffle its glassy surface and the sun fill it with throb spangle while its wave begin to lap and murmur around its leafless shore -- sun-spangle during the day and reflect star at night its only flower the wind and the snow its only visitor meanwhile the glacier continue to recede and numerous rill still younger than the lake itself bring down glacier-mud sand-grain and pebble give rise to margin-ring and plat of soil to these fresh soil-bed come many a wait plant first a hardy carex with arch leaf and a spike of brown flower then as the season grow warmer and the soil-bed deeper and wider other sedge take they appoint place and these be join by blue gentian daisy dodecatheon violet honeywort and many a lowly moss shrub also hasten in time to the new garden -- kalmium with its glossy leaf and purple flower the arctic willow make soft weave carpet together with the heathy bryanthus and cassiope the fairest and dearest of they all insect now enrich the air frog pipe cheerily in the shallows soon follow by the ouzel which be the first bird to visit a glacier lake as the sedge be the first of plant so the young lake grow in beauty become more and more humanly lovable from century to century grove of aspen spring up and hardy pine and the hemlock spruce until it be richly overshadow and embower but while its shore be be enrich the soil-bed creep out with incessant growth contract its area while the lighter mud-particle deposit on the bottom cause it to grow constantly shallower until at length the last remnant of the lake vanish -- close forever in ripe and natural old age and now its feeding-stream go wind on without halt through the new garden and grove that have take its place the length of the life of any lake depend ordinarily upon the capacity of its basin as compare with the carry power of the stream that flow into it the character of the rock over which these stream flow and the relative position of the lake toward other lake in a series whose basin lie in the same cañon and be feed by one and the same main stream the uppermost will of course vanish first unless some other lake-filling agent come in to modify the result because at first it receive nearly all of the sediment that the stream bring down only the finest of the mud-particle be carry through the highest of the series to the next below then the next higher and the next would be successively fill and the lowest would be the last to vanish but this simplicity as to duration be break in upon in various way chiefly through the action of side-stream that enter the lower lake direct for notwithstanding many of these side tributary be quite short and during late summer feeble they all become powerful torrent in springtime when the snow be melting and carry not only sand and pine-needle but large trunk and boulder ton in weight sweep they down they steeply inclined channel and into the lake basin with astounding energy many of these side affluent also have the advantage of access to the main lateral moraine of the vanish glacier that occupy the cañon and upon these they draw for lake-filling material while the main trunk stream flow mostly over clean glacier pavement where but little moraine matter be ever leave for they to carry thus a small rapid stream with abundance of loose transportable material within its reach may fill up a extensive basin in a few century while a large perennial trunk stream flow over clean endure pavement though ordinarily a hundred time larger may not fill a smaller basin in thousand of year the comparative influence of great and small stream as lake-filler be strikingly illustrate in yosemite valley through which the merce flow the bottom of the valley be now compose of level meadow-land and dry slope soil-bed plant with oak and pine but it be once a lake stretch from wall to wall and nearly from one end of the valley to the other form one of the most beautiful cliff-bound sheet of water that ever exist in the sierra and though never perhaps see by human eye it be but yesterday geologically speak since it disappear and the trace of its existence be still so fresh it may easily be restore to the eye of imagination and view in all its grandeur about as truly and vividly as if actually before we now we find that the detritus which fill this magnificent basin be not bring down from the distant mountain by the main stream that converge here to form the river however powerful and available for the purpose at first sight they appear but almost wholly by the small local tributary such as those of indian cañon the sentinel and the three brother and by a few small residual glacier which linger in the shadow of the wall long after the main trunk glacier have recede beyond the head of the valley have the glacier that once cover the range be melt at once leave the entire surface bare from top to bottom simultaneously then of course all the lake would have come coniferous forest of the sierra be the grandest and most beautiful in the world and grow in a delightful climate on the most interesting and accessible of mountain-range yet strange to say they be not well know more than sixty year ago david dougla a enthusiastic botanist and tree lover wander alone through fine section of the sugar pine and silver fir wood wild with delight a few year later other botanist make short journey from the coast into the lower wood then come the wonderful multitude of miner into the foot-hill zone mostly blind with gold-dust soon follow by sheepman who with wool over they eye chase they flock through all the forest belt from one end of the range to the other then the yosemite valley be discover and thousand of admire tourist pass through section of the lower and middle zone on they way to that wonderful park and gain fine glimpse of the sugar pine and silver fir along the edge of dusty trail and road but few indeed strong and free with eye undimmed with care have go far enough and live long enough with the tree to gain anything like a loving conception of they grandeur and significance as manifest in the harmony of they distribution and vary aspect throughout the season as they stand array in they winter garb rejoice in storm put forth they fresh leaf in the spring while steam with resiny fragrance receive the thunder-shower of summer or repose heavy-laden with ripe cone in the rich sungold of autumn for knowledge of this kind one must dwell with the tree and grow with they without any reference to time in the almanac sense the distribution of the general forest in belt be readily perceive these as we have see extend in regular order from one extremity of the range to the other and however dense and somber they may appear in general view neither on the rocky height nor down in the leafiest hollow will you find anything to remind you of the dank malarial selva of the amazon and orinoco with they boundless contiguity of shade the monotonous uniformity of the deodar forest of the himalaya the black forest of europe or the dense dark wood of dougla spruce where roll the oregon the giant pine and fir and sequoia hold they arm open to the sunlight rise above one another on the mountain bench marshal in glorious array give forth the utmost expression of grandeur and beauty with inexhaustible variety and harmony -lrb- illustration : view in the sierra forest -rrb- the invite openness of the sierra wood be one of they most distinguishing characteristic the tree of all the species stand more or less apart in grove or in small irregular group enable one to find a way nearly everywhere along sunny colonnade and through opening that have a smooth park-like surface strew with brown needle and bur now you cross a wild garden now a meadow now a ferny willowy stream and ever and anon you emerge from all the grove and flower upon some granite pavement or high bare ridge command superb view above the wave sea of evergreen far and near one would experience but little difficulty in ride on horseback through the successive belt all the way up to the storm-beaten fringe of the icy peak the deep cañons however that extend from the axis of the range cut the belt more or less completely into section and prevent the mount traveler from trace they lengthwise this simple arrangement in zone and section bring the forest as a whole within the comprehension of every observer the different species be ever find occupy the same relative position to one another as control by soil climate and the comparative vigor of each species in take and hold the ground and so appreciable be these relation one need never be at a loss in determine within a few hundred foot the elevation above sea-level by the tree alone for notwithstanding some of the species range upward for several thousand foot and all pass one another more or less yet even those possess the greatest vertical range be available in this connection in as much as they take on new form correspond with the variation in altitude cross the treeless plain of the sacramento and san joaquin from the west and reach the sierra foot-hill you enter the lower fringe of the forest compose of small oak and pine grow so far apart that not one twentieth of the surface of the ground be in shade at clear noonday after advance fifteen or twenty mile and make a ascent of from two to three thousand foot you reach the lower margin of the main pine belt compose of the gigantic sugar pine yellow pine incense cedar and sequoia next you come to the magnificent silver fir belt and lastly to the upper pine belt which sweep up the rocky acclivity of the summit peak in a dwarf waver fringe to a height of from ten to twelve thousand foot -lrb- illustration : edge of the timber line on mount shasta -rrb- this general order of distribution with reference to climate dependent on elevation be perceive at once but there be other harmony as far-reaching in this connection that become manifest only after patient observation and study perhaps the most interesting of these be the arrangement of the forest in long curving band braid together into lace-like pattern and outspread in charming variety the key to this beautiful harmony be the ancient glacier where they flow the tree follow trace they waver course along cañons over ridge and over high rolling plateaus the cedar of lebanon say hooker be grow upon one of the moraine of a ancient glacier all the forest of the sierra be grow upon moraine but moraine vanish like the glacier that make they every storm that fall upon they waste they cut gap disintegrate boulder and carry away they decay material into new formation until at length they be no longer recognizable by any save student who trace they transitional form down from the fresh moraine still in process of formation through those that be more and more ancient and more and more obscure by vegetation and all kind of post-glacial weathering have the ice-sheet that once cover all the range be melt simultaneously from the foot-hill to the summit the flank would of course have be leave almost bare of soil and these noble forest would be want many grove and thicket would undoubtedly have grow up on lake and avalanche bed and many a fair flower and shrub would have find food and a dwelling-place in weathered nook and crevice but the sierra as a whole would have be a bare rocky desert -lrb- illustration : view in the main pine belt of the sierra forest -rrb- it appear therefore that the sierra forest in general indicate the extent and position of the ancient moraine as well as they do line of climate for forest properly speak can not exist without soil and since the moraine have be deposit upon the solid rock and only upon elect place leave a considerable portion of the old glacial surface bare we find luxuriant forest of pine and fir abruptly terminate by score and polish pavement on which not even a moss be grow though soil alone be require to fit they for the growth of tree 200 foot in height the nut pine -lrb- _ pinus sabiniana _ -rrb- the nut pine the first conifer meet in ascend the range from the west grow only on the torrid foothill seem to delight in the most ardent sun-heat like a palm spring up here and there singly or in scattered group of five or six among scrubby white oak and thicket of ceanothus and manzanita its extreme upper limit be about 4000 foot above the sea its lower about from 500 to 800 foot this tree be remarkable for its airy widespread tropical appearance which suggest a region of palm rather than cool resiny pine wood no one would take it at first sight to be a conifer of any kind it be so loose in habit and so widely branch and its foliage be so thin and gray full-grown specimen be from forty to fifty foot in height and from two to three foot in diameter the trunk usually divide into three or four main branch about fifteen and twenty foot from the ground which after bear away from one another shoot straight up and form separate summit while the crooked subordinate branch aspire and radiate and droop in ornamental spray the slender grayish-green needle be from eight to twelve inch long loosely tasseled and inclined to droop in handsome curve contrast with the stiff dark-colored trunk and branch in a very striking manner no other tree of my acquaintance so substantial in body be in its foliage so thin and so pervious to the light the sunbeam sift through even the leafiest tree with scarcely any interruption and the weary heated traveler find but little protection in they shade -lrb- illustration : nut pine -lrb- pinus sabiniana -rrb- -rrb- the generous crop of nutritious nut which the nut pine yield make it a favorite with indian bear and squirrel the cone be most beautiful measuring from five to eight inch in length and not much less in thickness rich chocolate-brown in color and protect by strong down-curving hook which terminate the scale nevertheless the little douglas squirrel can open they indian gather the ripe nut make a striking picture the man climb the tree like bear and beat off the cone with stick or recklessly cut off the more fruitful branch with hatchet while the squaw gather the big generous cone and roast they until the scale open sufficiently to allow the hard-shelled seed to be beat out then in the cool evening man woman and child with they capacity for dirt greatly increase by the soft resin with which they be all bedraggled form circle around camp-fire on the bank of the nearest stream and lie in easy independence crack nut and laugh and chatter as heedless of the future as the squirrel _ pinus tubercula _ this curious little pine be find at a elevation of from 1500 to 3000 foot grow in close willowy grove it be exceedingly slender and graceful in habit although tree that chance to stand alone outside the grove sweep forth long curved branch produce a striking contrast to the ordinary grove form the foliage be of the same peculiar gray-green color as that of the nut pine and be worn about as loosely so that the body of the tree be scarcely obscure by it -lrb- illustration : the grove form the isolate form -lrb- pinus tubercula -rrb- -rrb- at the age of seven or eight year it begin to bear cone not on branch but on the main axis and as they never fall off the trunk be soon picturesquely dot with they the branch also become fruitful after they attain sufficient size the average size of the older tree be about thirty or forty foot in height and twelve to fourteen inch in diameter the cone be about four inch long exceedingly hard and cover with a sort of silicious varnish and gum render they impervious to moisture evidently with a view to the careful preservation of the seed no other conifer in the range be so closely restricted to special locality it be usually find apart stand deep in chaparral on sunny hill-and cañon-side where there be but little depth of soil and where find at all it be quite plentiful but the ordinary traveler follow carriage-road and trail may ascend the range many time without meet it while explore the lower portion of the merce cañon i find a lonely miner seek he fortune in a quartz vein on a wild mountain-side plant with this singular tree he tell I that he call it the hickory pine because of the whiteness and toughness of the wood it be so little known however that it can hardly be say to have a common name most mountaineer refer to it as that queer little pine-tree cover all over with bur in my study of this species i find a very interesting and significant group of fact whose relation will be see almost as soon as state : 1st all the tree in the grove i examine however unequal in size be of the same age 2d those grove be all plant on dry hillside cover with chaparral and therefore be liable to be sweep by fire 3d there be no seedling or sapling in or about the living grove but there be always a fine hopeful crop spring up on the ground once occupy by any grove that have be destroy by the burning of the chaparral 4th the cone never fall off and never discharge they seed until the tree or branch to which they belong die -lrb- illustration : lower margin of the main pine belt show open character of wood -rrb- a full discussion of the bearing of these fact upon one another would perhaps sugar pine -lrb- _ pinus lambertiana _ -rrb- this be the noblest pine yet discover surpass all other not merely in size but also in kingly beauty and majesty it tower sublimely from every ridge and cañon of the range at a elevation of from three to seven thousand foot above the sea attain most perfect development at a height of about 5000 foot full-grown specimen be commonly about 220 foot high and from six to eight foot in diameter near the ground though some grand old patriarch be occasionally meet that have enjoy five or six century of storm and attain a thickness of ten or even twelve foot live on undecayed sweet and fresh in every fiber in southern oregon where it be first discover by david dougla on the head water of the umpqua it attain still grander dimension one specimen have be measure that be 245 foot high and over eighteen foot in diameter three foot from the ground the discoverer be the dougla for whom the noble dougla spruce be name and many other plant which will keep he memory sweet and fresh as long as tree and flower be love he first visit to the pacific coast be make in the year 1825 the oregon indian watch he with curiosity as he wander in the wood collect specimen and unlike the fur-gathering stranger they have hitherto know care nothing about trade and when at length they come to know he better and see that from year to year the grow thing of far to the southward of the columbium at the end of the next summer return to fort vancouver after the setting in of the winter rain bear in mind the big pine he have hear of he set out on a excursion up the willamette valley in search of it and how he fare and what danger and hardship he endure be best tell in he own journal from which i quote as follow : _ October _ 26 1826 weather dull cold and cloudy when my friend in england be make acquaint with my travels i fear they will think i have tell they nothing but my misery i quit my camp early in the morning to survey the neighboring country leave my guide to take charge of the horse until my return in the evening about a hour 's walk from the camp i meet a indian who on perceive I instantly string he bow place on he left arm a sleeve of raccoon skin and stand on the defensive be quite sure that conduct be prompt by fear and not by hostile intention the poor fellow have probably never see such a be as myself before i lay my gun at my foot on the ground and wave my hand for he to come to I which he do slowly and with great caution i then make he place he bow and quiver of arrow beside my gun and strike a light give he a smoke out of my own pipe and a present of a few bead with my pencil i make a rough sketch of the cone and pine tree which i want to obtain and draw he attention to it when he instantly point with he hand to the hill fifteen or twenty mile distant towards the south and when i express my intention of go thither cheerfully set out to accompany I at midday i reach my long-wished-for pine and lose no time in examine they and endeavor to collect specimen and seed new and strange thing seldom fail to make strong impression and be therefore frequently over-rated so that lest i should never see my friend in england to inform they verbally of this most beautiful and immensely grand tree i shall here state the dimension of the largest i could find among several that have be blow down by the wind at 3 foot from the ground its circumference be 57 foot 9 inch at 134 foot 17 foot 5 inch the extreme length 245 foot as it be impossible either to climb the tree or hew it down i endeavor to knock off the cone by firing at they with ball when the report of my gun bring eight indian all of they paint with red earth arm with bow arrow bone-tipped spear and flint-knife they appear anything but friendly i explain to they what i want and they seem satisfied and sit down to smoke but presently i see one of they string he bow and another sharpen he flint knife with a pair of wooden pincers and suspend it off the wrist of he right hand further testimony of they intention be unnecessary to save myself by flight be impossible so without hesitation i step back about five pace cock my gun draw one of the pistol out of my belt and hold it in my left hand and the gun in my right show myself determine to fight for my life as much as possible i endeavor to preserve my coolness and thus we stand look at one another without make any movement or utter a word for perhaps ten minute when one at last who seem to be the leader give a sign that they wish for some tobacco this i signify that they should have if they fetch a quantity of cone they go off immediately in search of they and no sooner be they all out of sight than i pick up my three cone and some twig of the tree and make the quickest possible retreat hurry back to the camp which i reach before dusk i now write lie on the grass with my gun cock beside I and pen these line by the light of my columbian candle namely a ignite piece of rosin-wood this grand pine discover under such exciting circumstance dougla name in honor of he friend dr lambert of london the trunk be a smooth round delicately taper shaft mostly without limb and color rich purplish-brown usually enliven with tuft of yellow lichen at the top of this magnificent bole long curve branch sweep gracefully outward and downward sometimes form a palm-like crown but far more nobly impressive than any palm crown i ever behold the needle be about three inch long finely temper and arrange in rather close tassel at the end of slender branchlet that clothe the long outsweeping limb how well they sing in the wind and how strikingly harmonious a effect be make by the immense cylindrical cone that depend loosely from the end of the main branch no one know what nature can do in the way of pine-bur until he have see those of the sugar pine they be commonly from fifteen to eighteen inch long and three in diameter green shaded with dark purple on they sunward side they be ripe in September and October then the flat scale open and the seed take wing but the empty cone become still more beautiful and effective for they diameter be nearly double by the spreading of the scale and they color change to a warm yellowish-brown while they remain swing on the tree all the follow winter and summer and continue effectively beautiful even on the ground many year after they fall the wood be deliciously fragrant and fine in grain and texture it be of a rich cream-yellow as if form of condensed sunbeam _ retinospora obtusa siebold _ the glory of eastern forest be call fu-si-no-ki -lrb- tree of the sun -rrb- by the japanese the sugar pine be the sun-tree of the sierra unfortunately it be greatly prize by the lumberman and in accessible place be always the first tree in the wood to feel they steel but the regular lumberman with they saw-mill have be less generally destructive thus far than the shingle-maker the wood split freely and there be a constant demand for the shingle and because a ax and saw and frow be all the capital require for the business many of that drift unsteady class of man so large in californium engage in it for a few month in the year when prospector hunter ranch hand etc touch they bottom dollar and find themselves out of employment they say well i can at least go to the sugar pine and make shingle a few post be set in the ground and a single length cut from the first tree fell produce board enough for the wall and roof of a cabin all the rest the lumberman make be for sale and he be speedily independent no gardener or haymaker be more sweetly perfumed than these rough mountaineer while engage in this business but the havoc they make be most deplorable -lrb- illustration : sugar pine on expose ridge -rrb- the sugar from which the common name be derive be to my taste the best of sweet -- better than maple sugar it exude from the heart-wood where wound have be make either by forest fire or the ax in the shape of irregular crisp candy-like kernel which be crowd together in mass of considerable size like cluster of resin-bead when fresh it be perfectly white and delicious but because most of the wound on which it be find have be make by fire the exude sap be stain on the char surface and the hardened sugar become brown indian be fond of it but on account of its laxative property only small quantity may be eat bear so fond of sweet thing in general seem never to taste it at least i have fail to find any trace of they tooth in this connection no lover of tree will ever forget he first meeting with the sugar pine nor will he afterward need a poet to call he to listen what the pine-tree saith in most pine-tree there be a sameness of expression which to most people be apt to become monotonous for the typical spiry form however beautiful afford but little scope for appreciable individual character the sugar pine be as free from conventionality of form and motion as any oak no two be alike even to the most inattentive observer and notwithstanding they be ever toss out they immense arm in what might seem most extravagant gesture there be a majesty and repose about they that preclude all possibility of the grotesque or even picturesque in they general expression they be the priest of pine and seem ever to be address the surround forest the yellow pine be find grow with they on warm hillside and the white silver fir on cool northern slope but noble as these be the sugar pine be easily king and spread he arm above they in blessing while they rock and wave in sign of recognition the main branch be sometimes find to be forty foot in length yet persistently simple seldom divide at all except near the end but anything like a bare cable appearance be prevent by the small tasseled branchlet that extend all around they and when these superb limb sweep out symmetrically on all side a crown sixty or seventy foot wide be form which gracefully poise on the summit of the noble shaft and fill with sunshine be one of the most glorious forest object conceivable commonly however there be a great preponderance of limb toward the east away from the direction of the prevail wind no other pine seem to I so unfamiliar and self-contained in approach it we feel as if in the presence of a superior being and begin to walk with a light step hold we breath then perchance while we gaze awe-stricken along come a merry squirrel chatter and laugh to break the spell run up the trunk with no ceremony and gnawing off the cone as if they be make only for he while the carpenter-woodpecker hammer away at the bark drilling hole in which to store he winter supply of acorn -lrb- illustration : young sugar pine begin to bear cone -rrb- although so wild and unconventional when full-grown the sugar pine be a remarkably proper tree in youth the old be the most original and independent in appearance of all the sierra evergreen the young be the most regular -- a strict follower of coniferous fashion -- slim erect with leafy supple branch keep exactly in place each taper in outline and terminate in a spiry point the successive transitional form present between the cautious neatness of youth and bold freedom of maturity offer a delightful study at the age of fifty or sixty year the shy fashionable form begin to be break up specialize branch push out in the most unthought-of place and bend with the great cone at once mark individual character and this be constantly augmented from year to year by the vary action of the sunlight wind snow-storms etc the individuality of the tree be never again lose in the general forest the most constant companion of this species be the yellow pine and a worthy companion it be -lrb- illustration : forest of sequoia sugar pine and dougla spruce -rrb- the dougla spruce libocedrus sequoia and the white silver fir be also more or less associate with it but on many deep-soiled mountain-side at a elevation of about 5000 foot above the sea it form the bulk of the forest fill every swell and hollow and down-plunging ravine the majestic crown approach each other in bold curve make a glorious canopy through which the temper sunbeam pour silver the needle and gilding the massive bole and flowery park-like ground into a scene of enchantment on the most sunny slope the white-flowered fragrant chamoebatium be spread like a carpet brighten during early summer with the crimson sarcode the wild rose and innumerable violet and gilia not even in the shadiest nook will you find any rank untidy weed or unwholesome darkness on the north side of ridge the bole be more slender and the ground be mostly occupy by a underbrush of hazel ceanothus and flowering dogwood but never so densely as to prevent the traveler from saunter where he will while the crowning branch be never impenetrable to the ray of the sun and never so interblended as to lose they individuality view the forest from beneath or from some commanding ridge-top each tree present a study in itself and proclaim the surpass grandeur of the species yellow or silver pine -lrb- _ pinus ponderosa _ -rrb- the silver or yellow pine as it be commonly call rank second among the pine of the sierra as a lumber tree and almost rival the sugar pine in stature and nobleness of port because of its superior power of endure variation of climate and soil it have a more extensive range than any other conifer grow on the sierra on the western slope it be first meet at a elevation of about 2000 foot and extend nearly to the upper limit of the timber line thence cross the range by the lowest pass it descend to the eastern base and push out for a considerable distance into the hot volcanic plain grow bravely upon well-watered moraine gravelly lake basin arctic ridge and torrid lava-bed plant itself upon the lip of crater flourish vigorously even there and toss ripe cone among the ash and cinder of nature 's hearth the average size of full-grown tree on the western slope where it be associate with the sugar pine be a little less than 200 foot in height and from five to six foot in diameter though specimen may easily be find that everything great and small big tree -lrb- _ sequoia gigantea _ -rrb- between the heavy pine and silver fir belt we find the big tree the king of all the conifer in the world the noblest of a noble race it extend in a widely interrupted belt from a small grove on the middle fork of the american river to the head of deer creek a distance of about 260 mile the northern limit be near the thirty-ninth parallel the southern a little below the thirty-sixth and the elevation of the belt above the sea vary from about 5000 to 8000 foot from the american river grove to the forest on king 's river the species occur only in small isolate group so sparsely distribute along the belt that three of the gap in it be from forty to sixty mile wide but from king 's river southward the sequoia be not restricted to mere grove but extend across the broad rugged basin of the kaweah and tule river in noble forest a distance of nearly seventy mile the continuity of this part of the belt be break only by deep cañons the fresno the largest of the northern grove occupy a area of three or four square mile a short distance to the southward of the famous mariposa grove along the beveled rim of the cañon of the south fork of king 's river there be a majestic forest of sequoia about six mile long by two wide this be the northernmost assemblage of big tree that may fairly be call a forest descend the precipitous divide between the king 's river and kaweah you enter the grand forest that form the main continuous portion of the belt advance southward the giant become more and more irrepressibly exuberant heave they massive crown into the sky from every ridge and slope and wave onward in graceful compliance with the complicated topography of the region the finest of the kaweah section of the belt be on the broad ridge between marble creek and the middle fork and extend from the granite headland overlook the hot plain to within a few mile of the cool glacial fountain of the summit peak the extreme upper limit of the belt be reach between the middle and south fork of the kaweah at a elevation of 8400 foot but the finest block of big tree forest in the entire belt be on the north fork of tule river in the northern grove there be comparatively few young tree or sapling but here for every old storm-stricken giant there be many in all the glory of prime vigor and for each of these a crowd of eager hopeful young tree and sapling grow heartily on moraine rocky ledge along watercourse and in the moist alluvium of meadow seemingly in hot pursuit of eternal life but though the area place we might notice that no species of coniferous tree in the range keep its individual so well together as sequoia a mile be perhaps the greatest distance of any straggler from the main body and straight ditch and in the middle of this ditch a silver fir be grow that be now four foot in diameter and 380 year old as determine by cut it half through and count the ring thus demonstrate that the remnant of the trunk that make the ditch have lie on the ground _ more _ than 380 year for it be evident that to find the whole time we must add to the 380 year the time that the vanish portion of the trunk lay in the ditch before be burn out of the way plus the time that pass before the seed from which the monumental fir spring fall into the prepared soil and take root now because sequoia trunk be never wholly consume in one forest fire and those fire recur only at considerable interval and because sequoia ditch after be clear be often left unplanted for century it become evident that the trunk remnant in question may probably have lie a thousand year or more and this instance be by no mean a rare one but admit that upon those area suppose to have be once cover with sequoia every tree may have fall and every trunk may have be burn or bury leave not a remnant many of the ditch make by the fall of the ponderous trunk and the bowl make by they upturn root would remain patent for thousand of year after the last vestige of the trunk that make they have vanish much of this ditch-writing would no doubt be quickly efface by the flood-action of overflow stream and rain-washing but no root bowl occur in all the present sequoia grove and forest but as far as i have observe not the faintest vestige of one present itself outside of they _ we therefore conclude that the area cover by sequoia have not be diminish during the last eight or ten thousand year and probably not at all in post-glacial time _ be the species verge to extinction what be its relation to climate soil and associate tree _ all the phenomenon bear on these question also throw light as we shall endeavor to show upon the peculiar distribution of the species and sustain the conclusion already arrive at on the question of extension in the northern group as we have see there be few young tree or sapling grow up around the fail old one to perpetuate the race and in as much as those aged sequoia so nearly childless be the only one commonly know the species to most observer seem doom to speedy extinction as be nothing more than a expire remnant vanquish in the so-called struggle for life by pine and fir that have drive it into its last stronghold in moist glen where climate be exceptionally favorable but the language of the majestic continuous forest of the south create a very different impression no tree of all the forest be more enduringly establish in concordance with climate and soil it grow heartily everywhere -- on moraine rocky ledge along watercourse and in the deep moist alluvium of meadow with a multitude of seedling and sapling crowd up around the aged seemingly abundantly able to maintain the forest in prime vigor for every old storm-stricken tree there be one or more in all the glory of prime and for each of these many young tree and crowd of exuberant sapling so that if all the tree of any section of the main sequoia forest be range together accord to age a very promising curve would be present all the way up from last year 's seedling to giant and with the young and middle-aged portion of the curve many time longer than the old portion even as far north as the fresno i count 536 sapling and seedling grow promisingly upon a piece of rough avalanche soil not exceed two acre in area this soil bed be about seven year old and have be seed almost simultaneously by pine fir libocedrus and sequoia present a simple and instructive illustration of the struggle for life among the rival species and it be interesting to note that the condition thus far affect they have enable the young sequoia to gain a marked advantage in every instance like the above i have observe that the seedling sequoia be capable of grow on both drier and wetter soil than its rival but require more sunshine than they the latter fact be clearly show wherever a sugar pine or fir be grow in close contact with a sequoia of about equal age and size and equally expose to the with sequoia they mostly grow up beneath they like slender grass among stalk of indian corn upon a bed of sandy flood-soil i count ninety-four sequoia from one to twelve foot high on a patch of ground fresh ground prepare for they reception by fire thus fire the great destroyer of sequoia also furnish bare virgin ground one of the condition essential for its growth from the seed fresh ground be however furnish in sufficient quantity for the constant renewal of the forest without fire viz by the fall of old tree the soil be thus upturned and mellow and many tree be plant for every one that and apply in a few decade at the farthest all that will be leave of _ sequoia gigantea _ will be a few hack and scar monument two-leaved or tamarack pine -lrb- _ pinus contorta _ var_marrayana _ -rrb- this species form the bulk of the alpine forest extend along the range above the fir zone up to a height of from 8000 to 9500 foot above the sea grow in beautiful order upon moraine that be scarcely change as yet by post-glacial weather compare with the giant of the lower zone this be a small tree seldom attain a height of a hundred foot the largest specimen i ever measure be ninety foot in height and a little over six in diameter four foot from the ground the average height of mature tree throughout the entire belt be probably not far from fifty or sixty foot with a diameter of two foot it be a well-proportioned rather handsome little pine with grayish-brown bark and crooked much-divided branch which cover the greater portion of the trunk not so densely however as to prevent its be see the lower limb curve downward gradually take a horizontal position about half-way up the trunk then aspire more and more toward the summit thus form a sharp conical top the foliage be short and rigid two leaf in a fascicle arrange in comparatively long cylindrical tassel at the end of the tough up-curving branchlet the cone be about two inch long grow in stiff cluster among the needle without make any striking effect except while very young when they be of a vivid crimson color and the whole tree appear to be dot with brilliant flower the sterile cone be still more showy on account of they great abundance often give a reddish-yellow tinge to the whole mass of the foliage and fill the air with pollen no other pine on the range be so regularly plant as this one moraine forest sweep along the side of the high rocky valley for mile without interruption still strictly speak they be not dense for fleck of sunshine and flower find they way into the darkest place where the tree grow tallest and thickest tall nutritious grass be specially abundant beneath they grow over all the ground in sunshine and shade over extensive area like a farmer 's crop and serve as pasture for the multitude of sheep that be drive from the arid plain every summer as soon as the snow be melt the two-leaved pine more than any other be subject to destruction by fire the thin bark be streak and sprinkle with resin as though it have be shower down upon it like rain so that even the green tree catch fire readily and during strong wind whole forest be destroy the flame leap from tree to tree form one continuous belt of roar fire that go surge and race onward above the bend wood like the grass-fire of a prairie during the calm dry season of indian summer the fire creep quietly along the ground feeding on the dry needle and bur then arrive at the foot of a tree the resiny bark be ignite and the heated air ascend in a powerful current increase in velocity and drag the flame swiftly upward then the leaf catch fire and a immense column of flame beautifully spire on the edge and tint a rose-purple hue rush aloft thirty or forty foot above the top of the tree form a grand spectacle especially on a dark night it last however only a few seconds vanish with magical rapidity to be succeed by other along the fire-line at irregular interval for week at a time -- tree after tree flash and darken leave the trunk and branch hardly scar the heat however be sufficient to kill the tree and in a few year the bark shrivel and fall off belt mile in extent be thus kill and leave standing with the branch on peel and rigid appear gray in the distance like misty cloud later the branch drop off leave a forest of bleached spar at length the root decay and the forlorn trunk be blow down during some storm and pile one upon another encumber the ground until they be consume by the next fire and leave it ready for a fresh crop the endurance of the species be show by its wander occasionally out over the lava plain with the yellow pine and climb moraineless mountain-side with the dwarf pine cling to any chance support in rift and crevice of storm-beaten rock -- always however show the effect of such hardship in every feature down in sheltered lake hollow on bed of rich alluvium it vary so far from the common form as frequently to be take for a distinct species here it grow in dense sod like grass from forty to eighty foot high bend all together to the breeze and whirling in eddy gust more lithely than any other tree in the wood i have frequently find specimen fifty foot high less than five inch in diameter be thus slender and at the same time well clad with leafy bough it be oftentimes bent to the ground when laden with soft snow form beautiful arch in endless variety some of which last until the melting of the snow in spring mountain pine -lrb- _ pinus monticolum _ -rrb- the mountain pine be king of the alpine wood brave hardy and long-lived tower grandly above its companion and become stronger and more impose just where other species begin to crouch and disappear at its best it be usually about ninety foot high and five or six in diameter though a specimen be often meet considerably larger than this the trunk be as massive and as suggestive of enduring strength as that of a oak about two third of the trunk be commonly free of limb but close rather deeply the main furrow run nearly parallel with each other and connect by conspicuous cross furrow which with one exception be as far as i have notice peculiar to this species the cone be from four to eight inch long slender cylindrical and somewhat curved resemble those of the common white pine of the atlantic coast they grow in cluster of about from three to six or seven become pendulous as they increase in weight chiefly by the bend of the branch this species be nearly related to the sugar pine and though not half so tall it constantly suggest its noble relative in the way that it extend its long arm and in general habit the mountain pine be first meet on the upper margin of the fir zone grow singly in a subdue inconspicuous form in what appear as chance situation without make much impression on the general forest continue up through the two-leaved pine in the same scattered growth it begin to show its character and at a elevation of about 10000 foot attain its noblest development near the middle of the range toss its tough arm in the frosty air welcome storm and feed on they and reach the grand old age of 1000 year juniper or red cedar -lrb- _ juniperus occidentali _ -rrb- the juniper be preëminently a rock tree occupy the baldest dome and pavement where there be scarcely a handful of soil at a height of from 7000 to 9500 foot in such situation the trunk be elbow seemingly ill at ease and anxious like stubborn wrestler to rise again hemlock spruce -lrb- _ tsuga pattoniana _ -rrb- the hemlock spruce be the most singularly beautiful of all the californium cone remain to beautify the tree until the come of a fresh crop -lrb- illustration : storm-beaten hemlock spruce forty foot high -rrb- the staminate cone of all the conifera be beautiful grow in loveliest tree in the forest poise in thin white sunshine clothe with branch from head to foot yet not in the faintest degree heavy or bunchy it tower in unassuming majesty drooping as if unaffected revel in it no other of we alpine conifer so finely veil its strength its delicate branch yield to the mountain ' gentlest breath yet be it strong to meet the wildest onset of the gale -- strong not in resistance but compliance bow snow-laden to the ground gracefully accept burial month after month in the darkness beneath the heavy mantle of winter when the first soft snow begin to fall the flake lodge in the leaf weigh down the branch against the trunk then the axis bend yet lower and lower until the slender top touch the ground thus form a fine ornamental arch the snow still fall lavishly and the whole tree be at length bury to sleep and rest in its beautiful grave as though dead entire grove of young tree from ten to forty foot high be thus bury every winter like slender grass but like the violet and daisy which the heaviest snow crush not they be safe it be as though this be only nature 's method of put she darling to sleep instead of leave they expose to the bite storm of winter thus warmly wrap they await the summer resurrection the snow become soft in the sunshine and freeze at night make the mass hard and compact like ice so that during the month of April and may you can ride a horse over the prostrate grove without catch sight of a single leaf at length the down-pouring sunshine set they free first the elastic top of the arch begin to appear then one branch after another each spring loose with a gentle rustling sound and at length the whole tree with the assistance of the wind gradually unbend and rise and settle back into its place in the warm air as dry and feathery and fresh as young fern just out of the coil some of the finest grove i have yet find be on the southern slope of lassen 's butte there be also many charming company on the head water white pine -lrb- _ pinus flexili _ -rrb- this species be widely distribute throughout the rocky mountain and over all the higher of the many range of the great basin between the wahsatch mountain and the eastern flank of the sierra to which it be mostly restricted in grayish bush-like patch from the margin of the sage-plain to a elevation of from 7000 to 8000 foot a more contentedly fruitful and climate or soil it remain near the ground throw out crooked divergent branch like a orchard apple-tree and seldom push a single shoot higher than fifteen or twenty foot above the ground the average thickness of the trunk be perhaps about ten or twelve inch the leaf be mostly undivided like round awl instead of be separate like those of other pine into two and three and five the cone be green while grow and be usually find over all the tree form quite a marked feature as see against the bluish-gray foliage they be quite small only about two inch in length and give no promise of edible nut but when we come to open they we find that about half the entire bulk of the cone be make up of sweet nutritious seed the kernel of which be nearly as large as those of hazel-nut this be undoubtedly the most important food-tree on the sierra and furnish the mono carson and walker river indian with more and better nut than all the other species take together it be the indian ' own tree and many a white man have they kill for cut it down in its development nature seem to have aim at the formation of as great a fruit-bearing surface as possible be so low and accessible the cone be readily beat off with pole and the nut procure by roast they until the scale open in bountiful season a single indian will gather thirty or forty bushel of they -- a fine squirrelish employment of all the conifer along the eastern base of the sierra and on all the many mountain group and short range of the great basin this foodful little pine be the commonest tree and the most important nearly every mountain be plant with it to a height of from 8000 to 9000 foot above the sea some be cover from base to summit by this one species with only a sparse growth of juniper on the lower slope to break the continuity of its curious wood which though dark-looking at a distance be almost shadeless and have none of the damp leafy glen and hollow so characteristic of other pine wood ten of thousand of acre occur in continuous belt indeed view comprehensively the entire basin seem to be pretty evenly divide into level plain dot with sage-bush and mountain-chain cover with nut pine no slope be too rough none too dry for these bountiful orchard of the red man the value of this species to nevada be not easily overestimate it furnish charcoal and timber for the mine and with the juniper supply the ranch with fuel and rough fencing in fruitful season the nut crop be perhaps greater than the californium wheat crop which exert so much influence throughout the food market of the world when the crop be ripe the indian make ready the long beating-pole bag basket mat and sack be collect the woman out at service among the settler wash or drudge assemble at the family hut the man leave they ranch work speedily mark the joyful scene of they labor as the roasting-fire be kindle and at night assemble in gay circle garrulous as jay they begin the first nut feast of the season the nut be about half a inch long and a quarter of a inch in diameter point at the top round at the base light brown in general color and like many other pine seed handsomely dot with purple like bird ' egg the shell be thin and may be crush between the thumb and finger the kernel be white become brown by roast and be sweet to every palate be eat by bird squirrel dog horse and man perhaps less than one bushel in a the cañon between these extreme of size and habit a perfect gradation may be trace the largest i have see be fifty foot high eight foot in diameter and about seventy-five foot in spread the trunk be all knot and buttress gray like granite and about as angular and irregular as the boulder on which it be grow -- a type of steadfast unwedgeable strength chapter ix the douglas squirrel -lrb- _ sciurus douglasius _ -rrb- the dougla squirrel be by far the most interesting and influential of the californium sciurida surpass every other species in force of character number and extent of range and in the amount of influence he bring to bear upon the health and distribution of the vast forest he inhabit go where you will throughout the noble wood of the sierra nevada among the giant pine and spruce of the lower zone up through the towering silver fir to the storm-bent thicket of the summit peak you everywhere find this little squirrel the master-existence though only a few inch long so intense be he fiery vigor and restlessness he stir every grove with wild life and make himself more important than even the huge bear that shuffle through the tangled underbrush beneath he every wind be fret by he voice almost every bole and branch feel the sting of he sharp foot how much the growth of the tree be stimulate by this mean it be not easy to learn but he action in manipulate they seed be more appreciable nature have make he master forester and commit most of she coniferous crop to he paw probably over fifty per cent of all the cone ripen on the sierra be cut off and handle by the dougla alone and of those of the big tree perhaps ninety per cent pass through he hand : the greater portion be of course store away for food to last during the winter and spring but some of they be tuck separately into loosely cover hole where some of the seed germinate and become tree but the sierra be only one of the many province over which he hold sway for he dominion extend over all the redwood belt of the coast mountain and far northward throughout the majestic forest of oregon washington and british columbium i make haste to mention these fact to show upon how substantial a foundation the importance i ascribe to he rest the dougla be closely ally to the red squirrel or chickaree of the eastern wood ours may be a lineal descendant of this species distribute westward to the pacific by way of the great lake and the rocky mountain and thence southward along we forested range this view be suggest by the fact that we species become redder and more chickaree-like in general the farther it be trace back along the course indicate above but whatever they relationship and the evolutionary force that have act upon they the dougla be now the larger and more beautiful animal from the nose to the root of the tail he measure about eight inch and he tail which he so effectively use in interpret he feeling be about six inch in length he wear dark bluish-gray over the back and half-way down the side bright buff on the belly with a stripe of dark gray nearly black separate the upper and under color this divide stripe however be not very sharply define he have long black whisker which give he a rather fierce look when observe closely strong claw sharp as fish-hook and the brightest of bright eye full of tell speculation a king 's river indian tell I that they he the pine squirrel and when i ask a old trapper whether he know we little forester he reply with brighten countenance : oh yes of course i know he everybody know he when i be huntin ' in the wood i and most retire tenant of the dell they seek and find they all caress they tenderly bend they in lusty exercise stimulate they growth pluck off a leaf or limb as require or remove a entire tree or chapter xi the river flood the sierra river be flood every spring by the melting of the snow as regularly as the famous old nile they begin to rise in may and in June high-water mark be reach but because the melting do not go on rapidly over all the fountain high and low simultaneously and the melted snow be not reinforce at this time of year by rain the spring flood be seldom very violent or destructive the thousand fall however and the cascade in the cañons be then in full bloom and sing song from one end of the range to the other of course the snow on the lower tributary of the river be alaska lie beneath fold of ice come to the light farther down the range in large spring while those of the high sierra lie on the surface of solid granite expose to every change of temperature more have spread round about they that day chapter xii sierra thunder-storm the weather of spring and summer in the middle region of the sierra be usually well fleck with rain and light dusting of snow most of which be far too obviously joyful and life-giving to be regard as storm and in the picturesque beauty and clearness of outline of they cloud they offer striking contrast to those boundless all-embracing cloud-mantle of the storm of winter the smallest and most perfectly individualized specimen present a richly model cumulous cloud rise above the dark wood about 11 be swell with a visible motion straight up into the calm sunny sky to a height of 12000 to 14000 foot above the sea its white pearly boss relieve by gray and pale purple shadow in the hollow and show outline as keenly define as those of the glacier-polished dome in less than a hour it attain full development and stand poise in the blaze sunshine like some colossal mountain as beautiful in form and finish as if it be to become a permanent addition to the landscape presently a thunderbolt crash through the crisp air ringing like steel on steel sharp and clear its startling detonation break into a spray of echo against the cliff and cañon wall then down come a cataract of rain the big drop sift through the pine-needles plash and patter on the granite pavement and pour down the side of ridge and dome in a network of gray bubble rill in a few minute the cloud withers to a mesh of dim filament and disappear leave the sky perfectly clear and bright every dust-particle wipe and wash out of it everything be refresh and invigorate a steam of fragrance rise and the storm be finish -- one cloud one lightning-stroke and one dash of rain this be the sierra mid-summer thunder-storm reduce to its lowest term but some of they attain much larger proportion and assume a grandeur and energy of expression hardly surpass by those breed in the depths of winter produce those sudden flood call cloud-burst which be local and to a considerable extent periodical for they appear nearly every day about the same time for week usually about eleven o'clock and last from five minute to a hour or two one soon become so accustomed to see they that the noon sky seem empty and abandon without they as if nature be forget something when the glorious pearl and alabaster cloud of these noonday storm be be build i never give attention to anything else no mountain or mountain-range however divinely clothe with light have a more enduring charm than those fleeting mountain of the sky -- float fountain bear water for every well the angel of the stream and lake brood in the deep azure or sweeping softly along the ground over ridge and dome over meadow over forest over garden and grove linger with cool shadow depths chapter xiii the water-ouzel the waterfall of the sierra be frequent by only one bird -- the ouzel or water thrush -lrb- _ cinclus mexicanus _ sw -rrb- he be a singularly joyous and lovable little fellow about the size of a robin clothe in a plain waterproof suit of bluish gray with a tinge of chocolate on the head and shoulder in form he be about as smoothly plump and compact as a pebble that have be whirl in a pot-hole the flow contour of he body be interrupt only by he strong foot and bill the crisp wing-tip and the up-slanted wren-like tail among all the countless waterfall i have meet in the course of ten year ' exploration in the sierra whether among the icy peak or warm foot-hill or in the profound yosemitic cañons of the middle region not one be find without its ouzel no cañon be too cold for this little bird none too lonely provide it be rich in fall water find a fall or cascade or rush rapid anywhere upon a clear stream and there you will surely find its complementary ouzel flit about in the spray diving in foam eddy whirling like a leaf among beaten foam-bell ever vigorous and enthusiastic yet self-contained and neither seek nor shun you company -lrb- illustration : water-ouzel diving and feeding -rrb- if disturb while dip about in the margin shallow he either set off with a rapid whir to some other feeding-ground up or down the stream or alight on some half-submerged rock or snag out in the current and immediately begin to nod and courtesy like a wren turn he head from side to side with many other odd dainty movement that never fail to fix the attention of the observer he be the mountain stream ' own darling the humming-bird of bloom water loving rocky ripple-slope and sheet of foam as a bee love flower as a lark love sunshine and meadow among all the mountain bird none have cheer I so much in my lonely wanderings -- none so unfailingly for both in winter and summer he sing sweetly cheerily independent alike of sunshine and of love require no other inspiration than the stream on which he dwell while water sing so must he in heat or cold calm or storm ever attune he voice in sure accord low in the drought of summer and the drought of winter but never silent during the golden day of indian summer after most of the snow have be melt and the mountain stream have become feeble -- a succession of silent pool link together by shallow transparent current and strip of silvery lacework -- then the song of the ouzel be at its lowest ebb but as soon as the winter cloud have bloom and the mountain treasury be once more replenish with snow the voice of the stream and ouzel increase in strength and richness until the flood season of early summer then the torrent chant they noblest anthem and then be the flood-time of we songster 's melody as for weather dark day and sun day be the same to he the voice of most song-bird however joyous suffer a long winter eclipse but the ouzel sing on through all the season and every kind of storm indeed no storm can be more violent than those of the waterfall in the midst of which he delight to dwell however dark and boisterous the weather snow blowing or cloudy all the same he sing and with never a note of sadness no need of spring sunshine to thaw _ he _ song for it never freeze never shall you hear anything wintry from _ he _ warm breast no pinched cheeping no waver note between sorrow and joy he mellow fluty voice be ever tune to downright gladness as free from dejection as cock-crowing it be pitiful to see wee frost-pinched sparrow on cold morning in the mountain grove shake the snow from they feather and hop about as if anxious to be cheery then hasten back to they hiding out of the wind puff out they breast-feather over they toe and subside among the leaf cold and breakfastless while the snow continue to fall and there be no sign of clearing but the ouzel never call forth a single touch of pity not because he be strong to endure but rather because he seem to live a charmed life beyond the reach of every influence that make endurance necessary one wild winter morning when yosemite valley be sweep its length from west to east by a cordial snow-storm i sallied forth to see what i might learn and enjoy a sort of gray gloaming-like darkness fill the valley the huge wall be out of sight all ordinary sound be smother and even the loudest booming of the fall be at time bury beneath the roar of the heavy-laden blast the loose snow be already over five foot deep on the meadow make extended walk impossible without the aid of snow-shoe i find no great difficulty however in make my way to a certain ripple on the river where one of my ouzel live he be at home busily glean he breakfast among the pebble of a shallow portion of the margin apparently unaware of anything extraordinary in the weather presently he fly out to a stone against which the icy current be beat and turn he back to the wind sing as delightfully as a lark in springtime after spend a hour or two with my favorite i make my way across the valley boring and wallow through the drift to learn as definitely as possible how the other bird be spend they time the yosemite bird be easily find during the winter because all of they except the ouzel be restricted to the sunny north side of the valley the south side be constantly eclipse by the great frosty side of the larger branch where the snow could not fall upon they while two or three of the more enterprising be make desperate effort to reach the mistletoe berry by cling nervously to the under side of the snow-crowned mass back downward like woodpecker every now and then they would dislodge some of the loose fringe of the snow-crown which would come sift down on they and send they scream back to camp where they would subside among they companion with a shiver mutter in low querulous chatter like hungry child some of the sparrow be busy at the foot of the larger tree glean seed and benumbed come and go singing and diving at the same place for hour -lrb- illustration : one of the late-summer feeding-grounds of the ouzel -rrb- the ouzel be usually find singly rarely in pair except during stormy-petrel for duck go ashore as soon as they finish feed in undisturbed place and very often make long flight over land from lake to lake or field to field the same be true of most other aquatic bird but the ouzel bear on the brink of a stream or on a snag or boulder in the midst of it seldom leave it for a single moment for notwithstanding he be often on the wing he never fly overland but home and while thus buzz freely from fall to fall he be frequently hear give utterance to a long outdrawn train of unmodulated note in no way connect with he song but correspond closely with he flight
